Alteration of gene regulatory patterns associated with calcitriol, in the mouse data subsets (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9</a>) could indicate activation of compensatory mechanisms for spaceflight-induced skin barrier dysfunction, epidermal differentiation, and immune system alterations. L-asparaginase showed the strongest correlation with gene regulatory changes in these spaceflight-exposed mice (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9</a>). L-asparaginase is used in treatment of acute lymphoblastic leukemia and lymphoblastic lymphoma and exerts its action by depriving leukemic cells of circulating asparagine, resulting in DNA breaks, cell cycle arrest and cell death<sup><a href="#CR87" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR87">87</a>,<a href="#CR88" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR88">88</a></sup>. It is also an immunogenic protein and leads to skin rash, dry skin, and hypersensitivity reactions<sup><a href="#CR89" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR89">89</a></sup>. Similarly, there was strong positive correlation between gene regulatory changes in these spaceflight-exposed mice associated with use of the Vitamin A derivative tretinoin (all-trans retinoic acid)<sup><a href="#CR90" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR90">90</a></sup>, and two selective estrogen receptor modulators, tamoxifen and 4-hydroxytamoxifen<sup><a href="#CR91" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR91">91</a>–<a href="#CR94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR94">94</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par58">On the contrary, estrogen and diethylstilbestrol, a synthetic form of estrogen, showed a reversed pattern compared to the aforementioned drug targets (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9</a>). These drugs showed positive correlation with the expression changes in the RR-5 datasets, and the RR-7 25-day C3H/Hej data subset, and negative correlation in the other data subsets (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9</a>).</p></section></section><section id="Sec25"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Discussion</h2>
<p id="Par59">Skin is well-established as an essential organ for health on Earth, yet despite the frequency of dermatological issues in astronauts<sup><a href="#CR2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR2">2</a></sup>, the molecular response of skin to spaceflight is understudied. Here, we have performed a comprehensive study on the molecular impact of spaceflight on skin, with the aim of addressing gaps in the understanding of spaceflight associated skin health risks. Our analysis indicated intriguing gene regulatory changes occurring in the skin of spaceflight vs ground control mice during the MHU-2, RR-5, and RR-7 spaceflight experiments, that also show some overlaps with molecular alterations observed in astronauts.</p>
<p id="Par60">The genes exhibiting the highest variability across the datasets formed clusters associated with well-established physiological risks of spaceflight, notably immune dysfunction and muscle degradation (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1b</a>). Furthermore, the majority of genes showing significant differential expression across multiple missions in murine data were linked to cell cycle processes, particularly chromosome segregation (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a, b</a>). This suggests that skin mirrors common spaceflight signatures observed in other tissues, including dysregulation of cell cycle processes<sup><a href="#CR1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR1">1</a>,<a href="#CR19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR19">19</a></sup>. Further to that point, we see an enrichment of DDR pathways in some data subsets from radiosensitive BALB/c and C3H/HeJ strains<sup><a href="#CR75" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR75">75</a>–<a href="#CR77" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR77">77</a></sup> (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6</a>), which is also a well-established spaceflight response<sup><a href="#CR19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR19">19</a>,<a href="#CR74" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR74">74</a></sup>. These repair mechanisms may be activated to mitigate radiation-induced DNA damage and appear to remain active during the post-flight recovery process, as seen in the RR-5 mission where samples were collected 30 days after flight. This finding is consistent with reports of sustained post-flight DDR activation from the NASA Twins study<sup><a href="#CR19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR19">19</a></sup>. Notably, DNA damage in the skin has important implications for skin cancers including melanoma, of which astronauts carry a higher risk, although the direct correlation between spaceflight and melanoma is still under investigation<sup><a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">95</a>,<a href="#CR96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR96">96</a></sup>. Similarly, mitochondrial dysregulation is established as a key molecular signature of spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR78" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR78">78</a></sup>, and we see enrichment of an ISR pathway in all but two of the murine skin data subsets (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>), which may be driven by increased oxidative stress or DNA damage. ISR activation appears to be impacted by spaceflight duration, as the 25 day time points for RR-7 show a suppression of the ISR pathway<sup><a href="#CR1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR1">1</a>,<a href="#CR78" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR78">78</a></sup>. Using an explainable machine learning approach<sup><a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">32</a></sup>, we also observed the counteractive mechanism played by <em>D2HGDH</em>, which was generally upregulated in space flown mice (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7b</a>) indicating an increased need for breakdown of toxic D-2-hydroxyglutarate (D2H) molecules in mitochondria. In this model, the partner gene <em>RPLP0-PS1</em> codes for the ribosomal protein RPLP0 and is down-regulated in space flown mice. RPLP0 has previously been implicated in the top significant network for the mitochondria-mediated cycle of Alzheimer’s disease<sup><a href="#CR97" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR97">97</a></sup>. Low or absent levels of RPLP0 seem to limit the compensatory mechanism in the model (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7b</a>). If this occurs, build-up of D2H and suppression of certain enzyme functions can result, causing DNA and histones to enter hypermethylated states and activate oncogenes and inhibit tumor suppressors<sup><a href="#CR98" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR98">98</a></sup>. Build-up of D2H in the mitochondria may thus be related to cell changes induced by DNA damage from space radiation. Notably, gene regulatory changes associated with mitochondrial dysregulation and DDR were also reported in the skin data from the i4 mission<sup><a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a></sup>, corroborating these changes as key molecular hallmarks of spaceflight in the skin.</p>
<p id="Par61">The downregulation of collagen/ECM genes and the suppression of collagen/ECM-related pathways in the MHU-2 mouse mission, where spaceflight samples were collected shortly after landing (Figs. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2</a>b and <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5a</a>), mirror findings from previous studies where a reduction in collagen synthesis occurred under hypergravity conditions in cultured human fibroblasts<sup><a href="#CR99" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR99">99</a></sup>. In contrast, RR-7 mice (frozen in space) generally exhibited an upregulation of the same collagen/ECM genes (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5a</a>), consistent with results from the aforementioned human fibroblast study, which reported a 143% increase in collagen synthesis during microgravity<sup><a href="#CR99" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR99">99</a></sup>. Similar to RR-7, RR-5 also showed a trend of upregulation in collagen/ECM genes (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5a</a>). The opposing patterns between the MHU-2 mission and RR-5/RR-7 may arise from diverse factors owing to experimental design differences. Notably, the timing of sample collection introduces a critical variable, as seen in the MHU-2 mission where samples were collected less than one day post-flight, in contrast to RR-5, where collection occurred after a 30-day post-flight recovery, and RR-7, where samples were obtained directly in space. This temporal distinction implies that collagen suppression observed in MHU-2 mice may be influenced, at least in part, by the acute hypergravity response, a known factor in decreasing collagen synthesis<sup><a href="#CR99" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR99">99</a></sup>, and the stress-induced release of glucocorticoids<sup><a href="#CR100" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR100">100</a>–<a href="#CR102" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR102">102</a></sup>, rather than being solely attributed to the spaceflight environment. Moreover, differences in sex and housing arrangements further complicate the comparison; MHU-2 mice are singly-housed males, while RR-5 and RR-7 mice are multi-housed females, introducing additional variables that may contribute to the observed disparities<sup><a href="#CR100" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR100">100</a>–<a href="#CR103" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR103">103</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par62">Skin has an anatomically heterogeneous gene expression profile<sup><a href="#CR104" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR104">104</a></sup>. For instance, fibroblasts exhibit distinct gene expression patterns based on their positional identity in relation to the major anatomical axis<sup><a href="#CR105" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR105">105</a></sup>. This phenomenon in part explains the predisposition of different sites to different skin conditions<sup><a href="#CR104" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR104">104</a></sup>. Although several genes related to collagen synthesis and ECM were downregulated across all four MHU-2 experimental groups, some of these genes showed statistical significance in the dorsal group but not in the femoral group (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5a</a>). Likewise in the RR-5 mission, during terrestrial recovery, skin biopsies obtained from dorsal skin generally showed a higher reversal of gene expression pattern compared to those obtained from the femoral skin (Figs. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3</a>a and <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5a</a>). These changes may be indicative of the variations in gene expression profile, their adaptation response to space flight and re-adaptation to terrestrial environment, at different anatomical sites.</p>
<p id="Par63">Skin biopsies collected from i4 astronauts one day post-flight exhibited a marked downregulation of various collagen/ECM-associated genes in the dermal layer compared to pre-flight conditions (e.g., <em>SPARC, COL1A1, COL3A1, COL6A1</em>) (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5b</a>). However, certain genes, such as <em>SPARC</em> and <em>COL3A1</em>, showed an upregulation in one or both compartments of the epidermis (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5b</a>). Moreover, several other collagen/ECM associated genes (e.g. <em>COL14A1</em>, <em>COL15A1</em>, <em>MMP3</em>) were upregulated in both epidermis and the dermis (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5b</a>). As with the MHU-2 mice, the i4 astronauts’ skin biopsies were obtained one-day post-flight, so some of these effects may have been induced by hypergravity. Nevertheless, blood data obtained from the same i4 astronauts at 1-, 45-, and 82-days post-flight suggests that most gene alterations observed upon landing revert back to normal levels within 82 days following flight, so we may expect a similar response in the skin tissue (Supplementary Figs. <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">2c</a> and <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">3c</a>). Previously reported findings from the NASA Twins showed in-flight activation of collagen formation pathways and an increase in COL1A1 and COL3A1 proteins detected in the urine, indicative of structural remodeling<sup><a href="#CR19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR19">19</a></sup>. These changes, as with the majority of spaceflight-induced changes, were also reported to revert to baseline post-flight<sup><a href="#CR19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR19">19</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par64">Some of the changes reported by astronauts during and after spaceflight, such as dryness and contact dermatitis, point to an impaired barrier function<sup><a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a>,<a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">95</a></sup> our results support this hypothesis. <em>FLG</em> is a gene expressed in the stratum granulosum layer of the epidermis, during the epidermal differentiation process<sup><a href="#CR41" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR41">41</a></sup>. It encodes profilaggrin, a major component of keratohyalin granules found in the keratinocytes that is dephosphorylated and cleaved into filaggrin<sup><a href="#CR106" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR106">106</a>,<a href="#CR107" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR107">107</a></sup>. During conversion of keratinocyte cell membrane to cornified cell envelope, filaggrin aggregates and aligns intermediate filaments such as keratin 1 and keratin 10, within the cytoskeleton of keratinocytes, and subsequently a cross-linking process is initiated by proteins such as involucrin and locicrin<sup><a href="#CR108" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR108">108</a>,<a href="#CR109" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR109">109</a></sup>. This results in compactization and flattening of the corneocytes, one of the constituents of the skin barrier<sup><a href="#CR110" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR110">110</a></sup>. We identified <em>FLG</em> as one of the cross-mission genes in the spaceflight vs ground control mice data with downregulation across the majority of data subsets (all except RR-5 dorsal skin) (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a</a>). Additionally, we observed post-flight vs pre-flight downregulation of <em>FLG</em> in the epidermis of i4 astronauts (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5b</a>). Caspase-14, an enzyme encoded by the <em>CASP14</em> gene, cleaves filaggrin, breaking it down into free amino acids. These amino acids serve as integral components of natural moisturizing factors, contributing to the preservation of skin hydration and pH<sup><a href="#CR111" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR111">111</a></sup>. Additionally, they play a crucial role in antimicrobial defense, further contributing to the multifaceted functions of the skin barrier<sup><a href="#CR61" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR61">61</a>,<a href="#CR112" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR112">112</a>,<a href="#CR113" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR113">113</a></sup>. Similar to <em>FLG</em>, <em>CASP14</em> demonstrated downregulation in the majority of murine skin subsets (excluding RR-5 dorsal skin) (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a</a>), the epidermal layer of i4 astronaut skin tissue (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5b</a>), and JAXA hair follicles (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5c</a>). This observation is important considering the crucial role <em>CASP14</em> plays in skin barrier formation and its involvement in skin conditions such as contact dermatitis and atopic dermatitis<sup><a href="#CR53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR53">53</a>,<a href="#CR61" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR61">61</a>,<a href="#CR62" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR62">62</a>,<a href="#CR114" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR114">114</a></sup>. The link between filaggrin deficiency and dry skin, altered pH, atopic and contact dermatitis, and ichthyosis vulgaris is also well-known<sup><a href="#CR54" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR54">54</a>,<a href="#CR60" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR60">60</a>,<a href="#CR112" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR112">112</a>,<a href="#CR115" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR115">115</a>–<a href="#CR117" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR117">117</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par65"><em>CASP14</em> was also the driver of one of the two main QLattice models we found to be maximally relevant and minimally redundant among the cross-mission genes, resulting in a very simple model involving only <em>CASP14</em> and <em>S1PR1</em> with an AUC of 0.85 (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3d</a>). <em>S1PR1</em> was upregulated in most murine skin subsets (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a</a>), the in-flight vs pre-flight samples from the JAXA hair follicle study (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4b</a>), and in all compartments for the post-flight vs pre-flight i4 astronauts’ skin samples (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4a</a>). In the RR-5 mission, after a 30-day recovery, in-flight effects may have been reversed as <em>S1PR1</em> expression was downregulated (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a</a>), which is also consistent with downregulation in the post-flight vs pre-flight samples from the JAXA hair follicle study (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4b</a>). Downregulation of <em>S1PR1</em> has been reported to be necessary for activation of resident memory CD8 + T cells in skin<sup><a href="#CR35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR35">35</a></sup> and has previously been observed in CD8 + T cells from murine skin during response to Herpes Simplex Virus Infection<sup><a href="#CR118" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR118">118</a></sup>. While the S1P-S1PR axis has inhibitory roles in keratinocyte proliferation, it was also shown to contribute to the process of chemotaxis<sup><a href="#CR119" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR119">119</a></sup>. For instance, in Staphylococcus-aureus-stimulated keratinocytes; TNF alpha, IL-8 and IL-36<strong>γ</strong> release was induced via involvement of <em>S1PR1</em> and <em>S1PR2</em><sup><a href="#CR120" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR120">120</a></sup>. Higher expression levels of <em>S1PR1</em> and <em>S1PR2</em> observed in the skin of impetigo patients and changes in the distribution patterns of these receptors were also aligned with these findings<sup><a href="#CR120" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR120">120</a></sup>. Herpes Simplex Virus reactivation<sup><a href="#CR3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR3">3</a>,<a href="#CR4" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR4">4</a>,<a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">95</a></sup> and Staphylococcus colonization in the skin<sup><a href="#CR10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR10">10</a>,<a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a>,<a href="#CR121" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR121">121</a></sup> (which could also be influenced by breakdown of filaggrin altering pH levels<sup><a href="#CR122" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR122">122</a></sup>) have been widely reported in astronauts, therefore alterations in <em>S1PR1</em>, <em>CASP14</em>, and <em>FLG</em>, are of importance.</p>
<p id="Par66">At present, there is a lack of widely accepted countermeasures to effectively mitigate the damage caused to the skin induced by exposure to the space environment. Our analysis has identified drugs capable of altering similar molecular changes that manifest in the skin during spaceflight (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9</a>). Several drugs, including calcitriol, tretinoin, and tamoxifen showed significant positive correlation with regulatory changes in all data subsets from the MHU-2 and RR-7 missions except for the C3H/HeJ mice samples collected at the 25-day time point (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9</a>). Tretinoin, a vitamin A derivative is a retinoid known for its applications in treating acne and leukemia, exerts its effects through the activation of retinoic acid receptors, influencing various skin processes such as increased proliferation and inhibited differentiation of epidermal cells, increased keratinocyte turnover, increased collagen synthesis, inhibited UV radiation induced metalloproteinase activity, and melanin reduction<sup><a href="#CR23" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR23">23</a>,<a href="#CR123" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR123">123</a>,<a href="#CR124" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR124">124</a></sup>. Tretinoin treatment on reconstructed human epidermis was shown to induce loss of keratohyalin granules and reduce filaggrin and Keratin 10 expression<sup><a href="#CR125" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR125">125</a></sup>. Retinoids have also been observed to downregulate caspase-14 expression in in-vitro reconstructed skin models<sup><a href="#CR126" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR126">126</a></sup>. Dry skin and exfoliation are common side effects during its use<sup><a href="#CR123" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR123">123</a></sup>. Tamoxifen, a selective estrogen receptor modulator, has been shown to exhibit both agoniztic and antagonistic effects depending on the tissue<sup><a href="#CR94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR94">94</a></sup>. Its use in breast cancer patients is linked to cutaneous adverse events, including dry skin, itching, and occasionally radiation recall dermatitis and baboon syndrome (a Type IV hypersensitivity reaction)<sup><a href="#CR126" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR126">126</a>–<a href="#CR129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR129">129</a></sup>. Furthermore, tamoxifen induces androgen receptor expression in hair follicles, leading to tamoxifen-induced alopecia in some cases<sup><a href="#CR130" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR130">130</a></sup>, and abnormal hair follicles, epidermal atrophy and dermal fibrosis have been observed in rat models<sup><a href="#CR131" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR131">131</a></sup>. On the other hand, only olaparib, estrogen, and diethylstilbestrol (a synthetic form of estrogen) showed an opposing pattern, with significant negative correlation in all data subsets except for those from the RR-5 mission and the C3H/HeJ mice samples collected at the 25-day time point of the RR-7 mission (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9</a>). Estrogen plays a crucial role in skin health, influencing keratinocyte function, collagen production, moisture retention and telogen-anagen follicle transition<sup><a href="#CR132" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR132">132</a>–<a href="#CR135" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR135">135</a></sup>. In an explant model of fetal rat skin development, estrogen was shown to increase filaggrin expression<sup><a href="#CR136" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR136">136</a></sup>. Conversely, estrogen deficiency during menopause is associated with adverse skin changes such as loss of collagen, skin dryness and atrophy<sup><a href="#CR137" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR137">137</a>,<a href="#CR138" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR138">138</a></sup>. Overall, molecular alterations observed in the space flown mice and astronaut skin data show similarities with molecular effects associated with drugs like tretinoin and tamoxifen<sup><a href="#CR123" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR123">123</a></sup>, such as <em>FLG</em> and <em>CASP14</em> downregulation, while symptoms reported in astronauts including dry skin and dermatitis also resemble side effects associated with drugs like tretinoin and tamoxifen<sup><a href="#CR106" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR106">106</a></sup> (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9</a>). Topical moisturizers and emollients, as commonly used by astronauts<sup><a href="#CR15" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR15">15</a>,<a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">95</a></sup>, may help to counteract the physiological consequences of disrupted barrier function genes, and may also help to explain why results on impact of spaceflight on skin physiological parameters in astronauts have been mixed<sup><a href="#CR14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR14">14</a>,<a href="#CR15" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR15">15</a></sup>, despite physiological evidence of skin damage in space flown rodents<sup><a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">12</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par67">In conclusion, we have provided a comprehensive study on the impact of spaceflight on skin health. Currently, there is a gap in knowledge for how space radiation and microgravity affects skin biology, yet many of the common themes of spaceflight dysfunction emerged in our analysis, suggesting that skin could be an easily-accessible candidate for studying the biological impact of spaceflight. Our systems biology analysis revealed some genes of interest and potential mechanisms to investigate in the context of countermeasures that can be utilized in future studies. We believe with our study we have started to address the current gaps and provided some clues on how to potentially mitigate the adverse effects of the space environment to the skin.</p>
<p id="Par68">The rodent datasets used come from three different spaceflight experiments with a variety of confounding variables associated with differences in study design. This means that direct comparison between the experiments is challenging, but indeed this also presents an opportunity to hypothesize how these factors may influence biology, as done in this manuscript. A lack of physiological data, such as dermal thickness, and lack of individual skin layer data from the rodent datasets means that generated hypotheses relating to mouse physiology cannot be confirmed without follow-up investigations, but the comparison to astronaut data helps translate the results to human relevance. Lastly, gene expression levels differ between skin compartments so an identical fold-change value in a layer where the specific gene expression is generally low may not represent the same magnitude in a layer where the same gene’s expression is relatively greater. As such, for the i4 skin data we focused on changes in the compartments where the gene is established to be predominantly expressed.</p></section><section id="Sec26"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Supplementary information</h2>

<p id="Par69"><em>Communications Medicine</em> thanks the anonymous reviewers for their contribution to the peer review of this work. A peer review file is available.</p></section></section><section id="notes3"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Data availability</h2>
<p>For all the sequenced data underlying the plots in this manuscript the datasets used are publicly available via the NASA OSDR’s Biological Data Management Environment (<a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo</a>), including murine skin RNA-Seq datasets (OSD-238, OSD-239, OSD-240, OSD-241, OSD-254), microarray data from the JAXA hair study (OSD-174), and the JAXA CFE data (OSD-530). To create the UMAP plot in Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1b</a>, the normalized count data from all datasets was used. Genes where more than 25% of samples had identical expression levels were dropped, and from the remaining 75% of genes, we furthermore dropped genes with a variance of less than 1.7. The normalized count data used for the UMAP plot can be found at NASA Open Science Data Repository (OSDR), at the following links: <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-238" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-238</a>, <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-239" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-239</a>, <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-240" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-240</a>, <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-241" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-241</a>, and <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-254" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-254</a>. For each dataset, scroll down and click on Files -&gt; GeneLab Processed RNA-Seq Files -&gt; Normalized counts data. The source data for the rest of the figures are available in Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM7" class="usa-link">4</a>. Deposited data from the sequencing data from the NASA Twin Study can be found on the NASA Life Sciences Data Archive (LSDA) and the accession code is not available due to privacy concerns. The astronaut physiological data is also available through LSDA due to privacy concerns. LSDA is the repository for all human and animal research data, including that associated with this study. LSDA has a public facing portal where data requests can be initiated<sup><a href="#CR139" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR139">139</a></sup>. The LSDA team provides the appropriate processes, tools, and secure infrastructure for archival of experimental data and dissemination while complying with applicable rules, regulations, policies, and procedures governing the management and archival of sensitive data and information. The LSDA team enables data and information dissemination to the public or to authorized personnel either by providing public access to information or via an approved request process for information and data from the LSDA in accordance with NASA Human Research Program and JSC Institutional Review Board direction. The Inspiration4 data has been uploaded to two data repositories: the NASA Open Science Data Repository (osdr.nasa.gov; comprised of NASA GeneLab and the NASA Ames Life Sciences Data Archive [ALSDA]), and the TrialX database. Identifiers for publicly downloadable datasets in the OSDR are documented as follows: 1) Data can be visualized online through the SOMA Browser (<a href="https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Multiome/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Multiome/</a>), the single-cell browser (<a href="https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Multiome/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Multiome/</a>), and the microbiome browser (<a href="https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Microbiome/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Microbiome/</a>) and 2) for the PBMC data the data is available with OSDR accession ID: OSD-570 and the following link: <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-570/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-570/</a>.</p></section><section id="notes4"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Code availability</h2>

</div></div></section></section><section id="_adda93_" lang="en" class="data-availability-statement"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Data Availability Statement</h3>
<p>For all the sequenced data underlying the plots in this manuscript the datasets used are publicly available via the NASA OSDR’s Biological Data Management Environment (<a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo</a>), including murine skin RNA-Seq datasets (OSD-238, OSD-239, OSD-240, OSD-241, OSD-254), microarray data from the JAXA hair study (OSD-174), and the JAXA CFE data (OSD-530). To create the UMAP plot in Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1b</a>, the normalized count data from all datasets was used. Genes where more than 25% of samples had identical expression levels were dropped, and from the remaining 75% of genes, we furthermore dropped genes with a variance of less than 1.7. The normalized count data used for the UMAP plot can be found at NASA Open Science Data Repository (OSDR), at the following links: <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-238" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-238</a>, <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-239" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-239</a>, <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-240" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-240</a>, <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-241" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-241</a>, and <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-254" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OSD-254</a>. For each dataset, scroll down and click on Files -&gt; GeneLab Processed RNA-Seq Files -&gt; Normalized counts data. The source data for the rest of the figures are available in Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM7" class="usa-link">4</a>. Deposited data from the sequencing data from the NASA Twin Study can be found on the NASA Life Sciences Data Archive (LSDA) and the accession code is not available due to privacy concerns. The astronaut physiological data is also available through LSDA due to privacy concerns. LSDA is the repository for all human and animal research data, including that associated with this study. LSDA has a public facing portal where data requests can be initiated<sup><a href="#CR139" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR139">139</a></sup>. The LSDA team provides the appropriate processes, tools, and secure infrastructure for archival of experimental data and dissemination while complying with applicable rules, regulations, policies, and procedures governing the management and archival of sensitive data and information. The LSDA team enables data and information dissemination to the public or to authorized personnel either by providing public access to information or via an approved request process for information and data from the LSDA in accordance with NASA Human Research Program and JSC Institutional Review Board direction. The Inspiration4 data has been uploaded to two data repositories: the NASA Open Science Data Repository (osdr.nasa.gov; comprised of NASA GeneLab and the NASA Ames Life Sciences Data Archive [ALSDA]), and the TrialX database. Identifiers for publicly downloadable datasets in the OSDR are documented as follows: 1) Data can be visualized online through the SOMA Browser (<a href="https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Multiome/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Multiome/</a>), the single-cell browser (<a href="https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Multiome/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Multiome/</a>), and the microbiome browser (<a href="https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Microbiome/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://soma.weill.cornell.edu/apps/I4_Microbiome/</a>) and 2) for the PBMC data the data is available with OSDR accession ID: OSD-570 and the following link: <a href="https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-570/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://osdr.nasa.gov/bio/repo/data/studies/OSD-570/</a>.</p>
<p>Processing scripts used for rodent data are available via Github: <a href="https://github.com/henrycope/spaceflight-skin-transcriptomics" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://github.com/henrycope/spaceflight-skin-transcriptomics</a>.</p></section></section></section><footer class="p courtesy-note font-secondary font-sm text-center"><hr class="headless">
<p>Articles from Communications Medicine are provided here courtesy of <strong>Nature Publishing Group</strong></p></footer></section></article>
